Orel 1998: 177 cites both *camba and *gamba but notes *camba has "no traces ... in Balkan Romance". Huld is unsure of the direction of borrowing. Mann, Camarda, Meyer-Lübke, and Tagliavini see it as native. Mihaescu, Corominas, Orel earlier and Robles see it as borrowed from Greek (kampé). ... , ... , ... , ˌo l e ˈɑ s t e r , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , u lˠ ˈa ʃ t ə ɾ  $olive tree. Orel 1998(:485), following Çabej 1986(II: 244-5) argues that the variant ullastër is from Italian olivastro "influenced by ulli". I would note Old Occitan ullastre. Orel 1998(:485) however thinks the form ullaSHtër is from Latin oleaster. Çabej earlier (1960) preferred a native suffixation upon ulli "olive" in -shtër (-a-?), while Bonnet 1998(: 223) instead repackages this as a calque on the Italian. Note influence from an existing ullashtër onto the loan of Italian olivastro is also possible. Vatasescu 1997(:163) has ullashtër frm olivaster, which would be inherited only in Alb. Lack of Tosk rhotacism may be suspicious, opening the door that this may be a later loan. Candidates that marry phonological and historical plausibility include Old French chien cjɛn. Norman quien (cf Picard kien) is not an option here, nor is Occitan can. Seems a very likely Angevin loan. Note the vowel is not an issue for native though -- just the n rather than /ɾ/, which can in fact be explained as a Gheg to Tosk loan. Alternative from Latin after all ː < CANINVS -- I think Eric Hamp wrote on that somewhere (TODOǃ find that). ... , ... , ... , d ˈu r r ɑ t͡ʃ u m , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , d ˈu r ə s  $Durrës. This is a NOVEL PROPOSAL though foreshadowed by Bonnet 1998(:37)'sa remarks about the outcome -s- from what looks like a Latin and Greek /kʰiu-/, rather than the expected /c/ <q>. Italian likewise has Durazzo rather than expected Duraccio; Slavic Drač. The idea here is that the Latin Durrachium and perhaps the Greek Dyrrhakhium actually overlie a Proto-Albanian /tʃ/ (using this sound as it is what is used in Proto-Albanian as the reflex of PIE *kʷ before i/e by De Vaan 2018 and Orel 2000), rather than a /kiu/ or /kju/, which is plausible as this sound was lacking in both languages. Regarding the accent, once we no longer have the penultimate -I-, then the /u/ becomes antepenultimate, and the stress on it falls out easily from this proposal! ... , ... , d u r r h ˈa c u m , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , d ˈu r ə s  $Durrës. This is a NOVEL proposal though foreshadowed by Bonnet 1998(:37)'sa remarks about the outcome -s- from what looks like a Latin and Greek /kʰiu-/, rather than the expected /c/ <q>. Italian likewise has Durazzo (< Durratio) rather than expected Duraccio; Slavic Drač. The idea here is that the Latin Durrachium and perhaps the Greek Dyrrhakhium actually overlie a Proto-Albanian /tʃ/ (using this sound as it is what is used in Proto-Albanian as the reflex of PIE *kʷ before i/e by De Vaan 2018 and Orel 2000), rather than a /kiu/ or /kju/, which is plausible as this sound was lacking in both languages. Regarding the accent, once we no longer have the penultimate -I-, then the /u/ becomes antepenultimate, and the stress on it falls out easily from this proposal! Proposed etymology -- compound. Element 1: zero grade of PIE *dʰwœ́r "door" (whence via full grade PAlb dwōrā > døːra > Alb derë "door, pl. dyer). Second element a CPAlb hacum -- from h2ekʷ + yos, h2ekʷ as in h2ekʷ-eh2 whence Latin aqua "water", Lusitanian aqua "river", Protogermanic *awjō. Perhaps also implicit in akull "ice" (ak-ull). Original derivation in h2ekʷ (root of h2ekʷ-eh2) + -yos denominal adjectival stem (cf. a pseudo Latin *aquius)._Borrowed from Doric Greek dialects into Archaic Proto-Albanian_
... , ... , ... , d r ˌɑ n k ˈoː n e m , >* , >* , >* , d ɾ ã n k ˈu o̯ , >* , >* , d ɾ a ɡ ˈu a̯  $dragon, hero, or dragon slayer. Schumacher and Matzinger argue its from a Vulgar Latin drago:nem, not draco:nem. def nom sg and indef gen/dat/abl sg as dragoi. Pl dragonj, dragonjtë. Based on Gheg drangue, Orel 2000(:33) convincingly argues the source was not Latin dracōnem but rather drancōnem. De Vaan 2017: 1736 as drangua/drangue._Borrowed from Latin into Classical Proto-Albanian_

... , ... , ... , d ˌiː r ˈeː k t u m , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , d ɾ ˈɛ j t  $adj, direct, straight, right, just, correct. Orel 1998: 73; but Orel 1998(:17) also posits it as a lone from "Dalmatian (i.e. Western) Romance" along with trajtë and pajtoj. May be from V Latin drectus like Old French droit etc. De Vaan 2017: 1745 as drejt, no final ë. Bonnet 1998 has drejt and drejtë as separate etyma from the same source; Bonnet pp260, 348 on relation to dëreq, ndreq, dreqtë -- dëreq and drejtë attested in Buzuku, adverb dreq in Cham, Gjirokaster, Arbereshe, Arvanitika; Cabej 1958 (cited in Bonnet 1998: 260) attributes this to a local q/j alteration in the south. Treating the final -ë̈as orthographic here as it is not sounded in the standard and may function to signal the -a fpl form of the adjective. Comparison to Old Romanian derept -- Vatasescu 1997(:35), Cabej 1987: 312._Borrowed from Latin into Classical Proto-Albanian_

... , ... , ... , n ˈoː d u m , >* , >* , >* , n ˈy ø̯ , >* , >* , n ˈy e̯  $knot. Modern form is now nyjë, fem, singularized plural of nyjë, the plural of nye. Orel 2000: 55 etc. Vatasescu 1997(: 36) lists a feminine Gheg nye and nue vs. Tosk ne, nejë (which is masculine); Vatasescu indicates nyje too as a Gheg form. Landi apud Vatasescu argues that this Tosk ne is from nodus, the Latin singular, and nejë is from a Latin noda. She refers to Demiraj 1985 (pp133-4) "pentru dificultățile refericitoare la evoluția fonetică a lui NODUS...", and furthermore cites Bardhi's adjective "nyesh" as from a Latin NODOSVS. The masculine larushk refers to wild grapevine (Vitis sylvestri) or dark grape vine used to make raki, the feminine larushkë refers to a mutlicolored cows. ... , ... , ... , ɡ l ˈɑ n d e m , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , l ˈə n d ə  $acorn, gland (equated to French gland which means both by Bonnet 1998 -- "acorn" is the only meaning supported in Newmark 1998(:452)). Bonnet 1998(: 249) -- lëndë is Tosk; Cham and some Tosk have lënde; Gheg has lênn, lend, and land. Meyer 1891(:243) notes the plural lëndre in Cham/Arv in Greece, and Romanian ghindă, and Aromanian and Megl. glîndã. Çabej 1960 prefers a native etymology, connected to lënde "lens, lentil", which Bonnet 1998(:250) sees as more likely from Latin lentem itself. However, this latter lënde "lens, lentil" is not in Newmark 1998(; 452), which lists lënde as "matter, timber, acorn". ... , ... , ... , l ˌuː d ˈo n i oː , >* , >* , >* , l ˈu o̯ j , >* , >* , l ˈu a̯ j  $verb: to play, wriggle. Gheg: luej. Aorist lojta (Gheg luejta), ppcl luar. Lopuhaä 2015: 9-10. Bonnet 1998: 288; Meyer 1891: 248; Mihaescu 1993: 46. For Bonnet 1998, a case of internal -d- loss. However a rival internal etymology supported by Çabej 1986 VII: 227, Camarda 1864: 50, Orël 1998: 232. This item is argued to be related also to loz and lodër by Çabej, and to an Old Irish luaidim "to celebrate"; Bonnet finds these arguments unconvincing but does remark that most Romance abandoned lūdēre for iocārī (with exception in Portuguese loir). In many dialects, and in the Standard )(?) fjollë. Romanian făș(i)oară / fâșioară is of interest. Orel 1998(: 129) attributes it to a Middle Greek fakiólē (=eta) "handkerchief, napkin". Vatasescu also cites Kristophson 1988(p86) in doubting its Latin provenance as well. On the other hand, Meyer 1891(ː106, apud Orel 1998) separates fjollë from fqollë, attributing fjollë to fileōlum; Cabej 1962(ː32/192) likewise attributes it to fileˈolum without a written long o. This possibility is listed below. On another hand, Vatasescu 1997(ː383-4) provides a *folliol(us/a/um) as the source of fjollë specifically, apparently following Kristophson 1988(ː65) on this and providing Romanian fuior. These alternate possibilties (favoring feminines with tonic short o as the otherwise is the most likely to otherwise work out) are provided below for now. ... , ... , ... , w ˌi t ˈu l e ɑ , >* , >* , >* , >* , f ə t ˈu ʎ ə , >* , f t ˈu j ə  $one-year old she-goat. Variants: vëtulë, ftulë. Orel 1998: 15. ... , ... , ... , f l ˈɑ k u l ɑ , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , f l ˈa k ə  $flame. Plural flakëra (Gheg flakëna). Cf Romanian flacără, Italian fiaccola. Orel 1998: 98-9 as a "backformation" of a *flakull, itself from "dialectal" Romance flacula evinced in Romanian flacără, Italian fiaccola, replacing facula "little torch". Note however Campidanese Sardinian flake (Meyer 1891: 107; Bonnet 1998: 271). This seems close to the view of Burr 1975(:63-4). Çabej 1960 argued for a native Albanian formation based on the semantic richness, arguing for an elementary creation on an "expressive base". However, as Bonnet 1998(: 272), these semantic developments are all to parallel to those seen in Romance, especially in Romanian flacără and Istriot fagla. Bonnet 1998(: 272, 379) defends the original etymology of facula here as either a Late Latin or Albanian-internal metathesis, the latter from a *fakël -- though in all of these cases, the plural in -ëra (Gheg -ëna) would have had to be innovated pretty early. Admittedly this is a productive suffix (Orel 2000: 229). Proposal that the plural is actually the inherited form, from a Romance flacula (per Burr 1975, Orel 1998 etc) embedded below._Borrowed from Latin into Classical Proto-Albanian_

... , ... , ... , w ˌi n ɡ ˈi l i u m , >* , >* , >* , m ɲ̩ ɟ ˈi lˠ , >* , >* , m ə ɲ ɟ ˈi lˠ  $vigil, fast. Regular as mënjill per Orel 2000: 55. As vigilia > vëngjill/mëngjill in De Vaan 2017: 1739. Vatasescu 1997(:473) lists a *Gheg* mnjille reflecting Old Albanian (including Old Gheg, in Bogdani) mëngjille, as well as a mjille "ajunul unei sărbători”, corresponding to a Tosk vëgjile and vëngjillë. Currently using mëngjill as it seems to be the center of gravity (of sources examined thus far), and of course ɲɟ > ɲ would be the regular Gheg ND > N shift. It appears that in the REW (p712), nasals in the first syllable of Latin etyma in vigil- are unattested in Romance. Alternate PROPOSED etymology in invigilia considered below. Bonnet 1998(: 192) lists mëngjille, mnjille, vëngjille considers this to reflect Latin vigilia, with an analogical nasal under the influence of evangelium > ungjill "gospel" (perhaps also angelu > ëngjëll "angel"?); on p198 he remarks on this as one of six cases where a non-palatal lateral reflects /lj/. ... , ... , ... , m ˌɑ r i s l ˈuː k i u m , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , m l ˈy ʃ  $pike (fish). As mlysh or mëlysh, another variant is mlyç. Orel 2000: 109; Orel 1998: 271. May be contaminated with mëlysh, mlysh "idiot", or that word may be drawn from it? Meyer 1891(: 284) noted Italian merluzzo "cod" but Dashi 2013 does not have this; that Italian word is itself attributed to Occitan merlus, which is attributed to Lat. merula, but phonologically this could reflect a borrowing from a West Romance doublet. Çabej 1986 (:I 352) and Bonnet 1998(: 88) list it under Latin lu:cius or lu:ceus. The final consonant outcome is eyebrow raising in any case, though mlyç is closer to the expected outcome of -q. ... , ... , ... , ˌi m p e r ˈɑː t u m , >* , >* , >* , m̩ p ə ɾ ˈɛ t , >* , >* , m b ɾ ˈɛ t  $king. Variant: mpret. Gheg: mret. Arvanite: mbret. Orel 2000: 114 -- may be imparātus (he who defends). Or imperātus (he who rules) -- Orel 1998:252.  De Vaan 2017: 1743 as from imperātor, likewise Bonnet 1998(: 38, 44, 207*. Bonnet 1998(: 38) argues it can't come from the accusative imparātōrem and thus prefers the vocative-nominative; for the loss of -(ë)r, he asserts there was a truncation, as with praebyter > prift, with the -r- surviving in Tosk mbretëreshë and mbretëria (the -n- in Gheg for these, he asserts, is analogical). Line added below for that hypothesis. Old Albanian had mpëret -- Kuvendi in Arbënit (Vatasescu 1997: 426). On phrasiological equivalences between Romanian and Albanian for this word family, see Vatasescu 1997: 425-6. Matasovic 2024: 64._Borrowed from Latin into Classical Proto-Albanian_
